    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a81fbbf6fcb74cc78166a4f8aa8427f1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '78e4fc35-1699-41ea-a61e-15f7bc708a2f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                     <p>Energy Storage Research Center, Korea Institute of Science and Technology (KIST), Hwarang-ro 14-gil 5, Seongbuk-gu, Seoul, 02792 South Korea</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Han/Jonghyun">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Jun/Seong+Chan" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Seong Chan Jun<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d82019368" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Although sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) offer promising low-cost alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), several challenges need to be overcome for their widespread adoption. A primary concern is the optimization of carbon anodes. Graphite, vital to the commercial viability of LIBs, has a limited capacity for sodium ions. Numerous alternatives to graphite are explored, particularly focusing on disordered carbons, including hard carbon. However, compared with graphite, most of these materials underperform in LIBs. Furthermore, the reaction mechanism between carbon and sodium ions remains ambiguous owing to the structural diversity of disordered carbon. A straightforward mechanical approach is introduced to enhance the sodium ion storage capacity of graphite, supported by comprehensive analytical techniques. Mechanically activated graphite delivers a notable reversible capacity of 290.5 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 10 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, it maintains a capacity of 157.7 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> even at a current density of 1 A·g<sup>−1</sup>, benefiting from the defect-rich structure achieved by mechanical activation. Soft X-ray analysis revealed that this defect-rich carbon employs a sodium-ion storage mechanism distinct from that of hard carbon. This leads to an unexpected reversible reaction on the solid electrolyte surface. These insights pave the way for innovative design approaches for carbon electrodes in SIB anodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8184-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8184-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable batteries are indispensable in modern society, powering diverse applications on demand. Given the dwindling reserves and escalating cost of lithium, seeking alternatives has become imperative.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0001_R_d82019359e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) emerge as a leading contender to partially substitute prevalent commercial lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and broaden battery applications, given sodium's plentiful reserves, affordability, and performance similarities to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0002_R_d82019359e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Identifying anode materials with high capacity and consistent cyclability is crucial for their practical implementation.</p>
         
         <p>Graphite is frequently highlighted as a potential anode material for SIBs, drawing from its commercial success in high-performance LIBs due to its energy density and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0003_R_d82019359e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, natural graphite demonstrates subpar performance (35 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup>) with a limited reversible capacity for sodium ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0004_R_d82019359e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energ Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This issue stems from the larger ionic radius of sodium ions (0.102&nbsp;nm) compared to lithium ions (0.076&nbsp;nm), rendering the interlayer spacing of graphite (0.335&nbsp;nm) insufficient for sodium ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0005_R_d82019359e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Computational studies further indicate that sodium ion insertion into graphite is not thermodynamically favored.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0006_R_d82019359e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Several approaches have been reported to integrate sodium ions in graphite: 1) transitioning from carbonate-based solvents to ether-based electrolytes in SIBs, and 2) enhancing graphite-based anode materials through heteroatom doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0007_R_d82019359e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Mater. Chem. A">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although several studies on graphite SIB anodes have yielded significant findings, these anodes possess intrinsic limitations, including limited capacity, rate capability, and compatibility with diverse electrolytes.</p>
         
         <p>To address these issues, remarkable endeavors have been directed toward designing and fabricating expanded graphite (EG), enabling sodium ion intercalation within the graphite layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0005_R_d82019359e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#advs8184-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0008_R_d82019359e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prior research aimed at augmenting the interlayer spacing of graphite has achieved promising results to an extent. For instance, Wen et&nbsp;al. reported that chemically EG prepared by heat-treating graphene oxide possesses an expanded interlayer distance of 4.3 Å. This modified graphite displayed a capacity of 184 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA·g<sup>−1</sup> when used with a polycarbonate-based electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0009_R_d82019359e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun. J. Electrochem. Soc.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Past studies indicate that expanding the interlayer distance of graphite leads to improved reversible capacity, rate capability, and cycle stability. However, these enhancements required complex multi-step processes with low yields. Therefore, crafting a straightforward and scalable method for producing EG becomes vital for practical applications.</p>
         
         <p>From an electrochemical standpoint, while there have recently been numerous advancements in refining the performance of graphite anodes, the underlying reaction mechanism within these anodes remains unclear.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0010_R_d82019359e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The unidentified nature of the sodium-ion storage mechanism in graphite has stymied further advancements in graphite-based anode development. Exploring the reaction dynamics of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in graphite chiefly concerns the electrochemical attribute alterations stemming from structural modifications during recurring intercalation/deintercalation.</p>
         
         <p>Conversely, the reaction mechanism of sodium ions in hard-carbon anodes has been documented. The voltage profiles observed in the charge/discharge curves of hard carbon anodes comprise two distinct regions: (1) a sloping voltage range (1.0–0.1&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and (2) a plateau segment (≈ 0.1–0&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Numerous studies posit that the reaction mechanism depicted in the voltage profile during charge/discharge is contingent upon the evolving structural properties of hard carbon. Stevens et&nbsp;al. introduced the “house of cards” model, proposing that during the sloping voltage phase, sodium ions insert into the graphite layer, and in the plateau voltage region, nanopores fill with metallic sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0011_R_d82019359e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Liu et&nbsp;al. suggested a different “adsorption-insertion” mechanism. They proposed that the sloping voltage phase corresponds to Na<sup>+</sup> ion absorption at active sites, such as defects on the hard carbon surface, while the plateau voltage phase predominantly relates to sodium ion intercalation into the graphite layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0012_R_d82019359e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, Lu et&nbsp;al. reported that the ball milling of hard carbon increased the amount of defects and reduced the crystal size. These results, which demonstrated reduced capacitance and initial efficiency at low potentials, also supported the “adsorption-insertion” mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0013_R_d82019359e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, hard carbon presents challenges, including diminished rate capability due to its limited conductivity and structural stability. Therefore, there is an urgent need to elucidate the reaction mechanism of sodium ions in graphite during charge/discharge. This is the inaugural comprehensive study examining the sodium-ion reaction mechanism in graphite across the sloping and plateau voltage regions.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, the ball milling of graphite has increased its viability as a host for sodium ions by controlling its particle size, surface area, and interlayer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0014_R_d82019359e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Nanoscale">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Using the ball milling technique, we introduced a unique mechanical approach to prepare activated graphite. The physicochemical properties of the graphite were modulated based on the ball-milling duration. We examined the sodium ion storage behavior of this mechanically activated graphite, focusing on alterations in its structural properties. The activated graphite showcased a capacity of 245 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, it maintained a capacity of 130 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> at an elevated current density of 1 A·g<sup>−1</sup> and demonstrated outstanding cycling stability, sustaining 130 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> over 3000 cycles at a current density of 1 A·g<sup>−1</sup>. We employed near-edge X-ray absorption fine structure (NEXAFS) spectroscopy using synchrotron X-rays to precisely discern the reaction mechanism within the sloping voltage region. These findings highlight an unforeseen reversible reaction at the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) surface. Ex situ and in situ analyses revealed varied surface-bulk reactions during ball milling. This newly posited reaction mechanism offers valuable perspectives for crafting and advancing high-performance graphite anodes for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8184-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8184-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs8184-sec-0030-title">2.1 Changes in the Structure and the Chemical Properties of Graphite</h3>
            
            <p>Ball milling influences the structural and physical attributes of graphite, including particle size and interlayer distance, alongside its chemical properties. Before delving into the impact of these modifications on the sodium-ion storage capacity, we characterized the shifts in physicochemical properties corresponding to different ball-milling durations. The samples are labeled as x h-MG, where “x” represents the ball-milling duration. For instance, “12&nbsp;h-MG” signifies graphite milled for 12&nbsp;h (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1A</a>). Crystal structure variations with ball milling time were assessed using powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1B</a>). Pristine graphite exhibited a pronounced diffraction peak at ≈26.12°, correlating to the (002) plane of graphite (PDF card No. 00-049-1642).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0015_R_d82019359e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Powder Diffr.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With extended ball milling time, the intensity of this diffraction peak decreased. The diminished intensity and broadening of the (002) peak post-milling signified a reduction in the crystallinity and particle grain size of the graphite. The diffraction peaks in the 8&nbsp;h-MG sample were notably fainter than those of pristine graphite. This suggests the emergence of a disordered carbon framework in the graphite, lacking a long-range order.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/358ce041-de69-4c92-8534-078287392ad1/advs8184-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/358ce041-de69-4c92-8534-078287392ad1/advs8184-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ee4f5150-bbbd-44b3-81c8-b4627f731331/advs8184-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/358ce041-de69-4c92-8534-078287392ad1/advs8184-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) Schematic illustrations of pristine and ball-milled graphite, along with interlayer structure representations before and after ball milling. B) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, C) Raman spectra, and D) near-edge X-ray absorption fine structure (NEXAFS) measurements for pristine graphite and ball-milled graphite at different milling times. E) d-spacing variation, F) crystallite size along the c-axis (<i>L</i><sub><i>c</i></sub>) calculated using the Scherrer equation from XRD, G) I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio in Raman spectra, and crystallite size along the a-axis (<i>L</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>) over milling time determined from XRD.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The dotted lines in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1B</a> highlight the normalized intensities of the XRD peaks, illustrating variations in peak contours. Importantly, the (002) peak intensities decreased and subsequently flattened with increasing ball-milling times; this is attributable to the increased defect density and decreased crystallinity because a high impact and shear force can deteriorate the C═C/C─C bonds in graphite. After 12&nbsp;h of milling, a noticeable shift in the (002) peak toward a lower angle (26.4°) is observed. This shift reflects the swift amorphization of the structure and an increase in the interlayer spacing of the graphite layers. A distinct peak at 30.12°, observed post 8&nbsp;h of milling, was ascribed to an unintentional ZrO<sub>2</sub> impurity, likely sourced from the zirconia crucible and balls.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0016_R_d82019359e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Metall. Mater. Trans. A">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XRD data determined the crystallite size along the c-axis of graphite, <i>L</i><sub><i>c</i></sub>, and the interlayer spacing to elucidate the variations in particle size and crystalline structure. <i>L<sub>c</sub></i> was computed using the Scherrer equation given by (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1E</a>):<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0017_R_d82019359e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="advs8184-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6a9d979a-34bb-4487-badb-a2cd17708678/advs8184-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs8184:advs8184-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/advs8184-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>L</mi>
                                 <mi>c</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mn>0.9</mn>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mi>λ</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>β</mi>
                                    <mi>cos</mi>
                                    <mi>θ</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\ {{L}_c} = \ 0.9\frac{\lambda }{{\beta \cos \theta }}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where λ represents the X-ray wavelength, θ is the Bragg angle, and β denotes the full width at half intensity of the diffraction peaks. Initially, pristine graphite exhibited an <i>L<sub>c</sub></i> of 178.8&nbsp;nm. However, after 4&nbsp;h of milling, the <i>L<sub>c</sub></i> of 4&nbsp;h-MG plummeted to 40.28&nbsp;nm. Beyond 8&nbsp;h of milling, the <i>L<sub>c</sub></i> value was stabilized and remained constant. The <i>L<sub>c</sub></i> values for 8&nbsp;h-MG, 12&nbsp;h-MG, and 24&nbsp;h-MG were calculated at 14.58, 13.09, and 15.57&nbsp;nm, respectively. Approximately 80% of the total <i>L<sub>c</sub></i> reduction occurred within the first 4&nbsp;h of milling, with 20% alteration observed thereafter. The high-energy ball milling can easily disrupt the innate order of the (002) basal planes owing to the weak bonds between these planes, reducing even large initial crystals to significantly reduced sizes post-milling.</div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The interlayer distance of the (002) planes represents the spacing between the graphite layers and was determined from the position of the (002) diffraction peak using Bragg's equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0018_R_d82019359e704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Compos. Sci. Technol.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="advs8184-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/341e0d01-2760-4b81-826c-9211e3fbb280/advs8184-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs8184:advs8184-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/advs8184-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              </mrow>
                              <mi>sin</mi>
                              <mi>θ</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mi>n</mi>
                              <mi>λ</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}2{\mathrm{d\ }}\sin {{\theta}} = \ n\lambda \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>In this equation, “d” represents the average interlayer spacing, “θ” represents the Bragg angle, and “λ” represents the X-ray wavelength. Up to 4&nbsp;h of milling, the d-spacing remained relatively constant, with the d-spacings of pristine graphite at 0.341&nbsp;nm and 4&nbsp;h-MG at 0.343&nbsp;nm. However, a sharp increase in d-spacing becomes evident after 8&nbsp;h of milling. The d-spacings for 8 and 12&nbsp;h-MG were measured to be 0.361 and 0.363&nbsp;nm, respectively. This expansion in interlayer distance was attributed to the insertion of interstitial carbon atoms between the aromatic planes of graphite due to mechanical milling. The ball milling equipment utilized in this study seemed to have reached its mechanical limit after 12&nbsp;h, resulting in no additional grinding effect. For instance, the d-spacing of 24&nbsp;h-MG, at 0.359&nbsp;nm, was slightly lower than that of the 12&nbsp;h-MG. This could be due to the high-temperature conditions within the ceramic vessels from frictional heat, potentially causing structural recovery at the atomic level of the milled graphite. The particle size rapidly decreased after 1&nbsp;h of ball milling, while the inner crystal structure, as indicated by the d-spacing, changed after 4&nbsp;h.</p>
            
            <p>Raman spectroscopy was conducted to investigate the degree of disorder and amorphization of the milled graphite, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1C</a>. Pristine graphite exhibits a prominent G peak (≈1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>) corresponding to sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbon-carbon bonds from the first-order scattering of the E<sub>2g</sub> phonon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0019_R_d82019359e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the weak D peak (≈1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>) was associated with lattice distortions and defects within graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0020_R_d82019359e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensities of the G and D peaks for pristine graphite indicate a well-ordered structure with a defined crystal size. The D peak for the milled graphite was significantly larger than that for the pristine graphite, pointing to crystal size reduction and defect formation within the hexagonal graphite structure. The Raman spectra of graphite milled for 8&nbsp;h are markedly different from those of pristine graphite. The intensity of the D peak surpasses that of the G peak, and both peaks broaden with a significant intensity decrease. The inverted intensity ratio between the D and G peaks was attributed to the considerable increase in the disorder of graphite caused by ball milling. This milling introduced lattice defects and exposed edges that inhibited lattice vibrations. The growing disorder degree was quantified using the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio. The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value escalated from 0.23 (pristine graphite) to its highest at 1.15 (12&nbsp;h-MG), subsequently decreasing to 0.97 after 24&nbsp;h of milling. The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio rise is attributed to the formation of structural defects and lattice disorder during milling; thus, 12&nbsp;h-MG could possess the highest interlayer distance and lowest crystalline size.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Furthermore, NEXAFS spectroscopy was utilized to examine alterations in the bonding structure of the milled graphite. Typically, graphite consists of stacked parallel carbon layers with different chemical bonds in the in-plane and in-plane directions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0021_R_d82019359e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Each carbon atom forms a strong covalent sigma (σ) bond with three adjacent carbon atoms within the plane. Furthermore, weak van der Waals pi (π) bonds connect the carbon planes in the z-direction. These two bond types were clearly distinguished in the NEXAFS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1D</a>). NEXAFS is also instrumental in exploring unoccupied electronic states and verifying the presence or absence of specific bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0022_R_d82019359e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Phys. Rev. B">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The spectrum for pristine graphite (black line) showed two predominant peaks at ≈285 and 292&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the π<sup>*</sup> and σ<sup>*</sup> bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0023_R_d82019359e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Electron Spectrosc. Relat. Phenom.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this study, the crystallinity of the milled graphite was compromised due to the disruption of chemical bonds from mechanical stress. Post 4-h milling, the intensity of the σ<sup>*</sup> bonds appeared relatively lower than that of the π<sup>*</sup> bonds. This indicated that the weak van der Waals interactions between the carbon planes were compromised before the in-plane bonds. After 12&nbsp;h of milling, the peak intensities for the π<sup>*</sup> bonds decreased owing to the covalent sigma bond breakdown. Furthermore, the peak position of π<sup>*</sup> bonds slightly shifted upward. This was attributed to alterations in the bond distance for covalent bonding caused by the disruption of sp<sup>2</sup> bonding among carbon atoms. The introduction of zirconia and oxygen atoms to the carbon–carbon bonds might also account for this peak shift.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0024_R_d82019359e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak intensity of the σ<sup>*</sup> bond consistently diminished beyond 4&nbsp;h of milling, indicating the breakdown of interplane bonds. The new peak at 288.8&nbsp;eV was attributed to the formation of oxygen functional groups onto milled graphite. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1F</a>, the in-plane crystallite size, <i>L</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>, denoted the crystallite size along the a-axis. This value was derived from the Raman spectra using the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio according to the following equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0020_R_d82019359e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="advs8184-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5308f1a8-a7fc-4879-96ed-233c696506e4/advs8184-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs8184:advs8184-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/advs8184-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>L</mi>
                                 <mi>a</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>2.4</mn>
                                    <mo>×</mo>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mn>10</mn>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mn>10</mn>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfenced>
                              <msubsup>
                                 <mi>λ</mi>
                                 <mi>l</mi>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                              </msubsup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>I</mi>
                                          <mi>D</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>I</mi>
                                          <mi>G</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mo>−</mo>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\ {{L}_a} = \ \left( {2.4 \times {{{10}}^{ - 10}}} \right)\lambda _l^4{{\left( {\frac{{{{I}_D}}}{{{{I}_G}}}} \right)}^{ - 1}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where λ<sub><i>l</i></sub> (nm) is the wavelength of the Raman emissions, while I<sub>D</sub> and I<sub>G</sub> are the intensities of the D and G peaks, respectively. The variation in <i>L<sub>a</sub></i> (calculated from the Raman spectra) was consistent with the variation in <i>L<sub>c</sub></i> (calculated from the XRD data). This correlation further corroborated that the primary change in crystallite size occurred during the initial stages of ball milling.</div>
            
            <p>The relative peak intensity ratio (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1G</a>) of the π<sup>*</sup> and σ<sup>*</sup> bonds increased with prolonged milling. This suggests a disruption in the interlayer bonding between the graphite layers. Moreover, the chemical bonding and elemental composition of milled graphite were characterized using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Full-range XPS scans of pristine graphite and 4, 12, and 24&nbsp;h-MG graphite are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). These results confirm that the milled graphite primarily consisted of carbon and oxygen.</p>
            
            <p>Based on the material characterizations, the mechanical activation process of graphite through ball milling is divided into three steps, defined by the primary reaction occurring during each interval. This classification drew upon XRD, Raman spectroscopy, and NEXAFS spectroscopy data. The steps were as follows: i) Step 1 (0–4&nbsp;h milling), decrease in graphite particle size; ii) step 2 (4–12&nbsp;h milling), augmentation of disorder and distortion of the inner crystalline structure; and iii) step 3 (12–24&nbsp;h milling), structural saturation and minor restoration. Step 1 entailed exerting mechanical force primarily to reduce particle size, as evidenced by XRD and Raman spectroscopy findings. Step 2 involved disruption of the crystalline structure. The diminished surface area in this stage resulted from particle recombination. This recombination manifested as mere physical adhesion of the fragmented particles without restoration of the long-range crystalline order. The variances in the reactions between steps 1 and 2 were underscored by shifts in the surface area of graphite.</p>
            
            <p>Morphological and microstructural changes with milling time were investigated using scanning electron microscopy (SEM). The surface morphology of pristine graphite exhibited a typical layered configuration with a smooth surface (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2A</a>). After ball milling for 4&nbsp;h, the graphite particle grain size became discernible, revealing grains with an average diameter of 100–200&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2B</a>) Furthermore, nanosized flake-like remnants of the layered structures are observed at the particle edges. The graphite powder underwent a morphological transformation with increasing milling time, while the 12&nbsp;h-MG exhibited an agglomerated granular structure with a rough surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2C</a>). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) contrasts the morphological features of the 4 and 12&nbsp;h-MG. The images confirmed that 4&nbsp;h-MG possessed a porous structure, whereas 12&nbsp;h-MG comprised aggregated particles.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3a563940-5ee0-4bb8-807b-ec00c4447225/advs8184-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3a563940-5ee0-4bb8-807b-ec00c4447225/advs8184-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/69fdfe8b-8984-49a2-8220-0e7fc2761b9b/advs8184-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3a563940-5ee0-4bb8-807b-ec00c4447225/advs8184-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological variations of pristine and ball-milled graphite. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of A) pristine graphite, B) 4&nbsp;h-MG, and C) 12&nbsp;h-MG. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) images of D) pristine graphite, E) 4&nbsp;h-MG, and F) 12&nbsp;h-MG. G–I) Interlayer spacing of the aforementioned samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was employed to further analyze the microstructural properties of graphite at the nanometer scale, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2D</a>, revealing the highly crystalline layered structure of pristine graphite. After 4&nbsp;h of milling, the graphite powder was fragmented into small particles, displaying irregular interlayer spacing. This is attributed to the mechanical disruptions that dislodged carbon atoms from their positions during the ball milling process. These displaced atoms repulsively interacted with the remaining carbon atoms, forming a wrinkled morphology (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2E</a>). The 4&nbsp;h-MG exhibited disordered regions alongside a local fine-layered structure. However, the degree of irregularity of the graphite structure became more pronounced with increasing milling time up to 12&nbsp;h. The local fine-layered structure vanished in the 12&nbsp;h-MG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2F</a>). Moreover, the 12&nbsp;h-MG displayed high internal porosity and highly disordered carbon atoms in its structure. These microstructural alterations influence the interlayer spacing of graphite, which was comprehensively analyzed in this study. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2G</a> shows the characteristic layered structure of graphite, with a consistent interlayer spacing of 0.33&nbsp;nm. The 4&nbsp;h-MG exhibited an irregular structure with an interlayer spacing of 0.35&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2H</a>). Furthermore, the interlayer spacing of the 12&nbsp;h-MG (0.36&nbsp;nm) surpassed that of the 4&nbsp;h-MG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0002">2I</a>). This indicates that the inherent morphology of graphite transformed after 4&nbsp;h of ball milling, with subsequent alterations in its internal crystalline structure by the 12&nbsp;h mark. The disintegration of the crystal structure and the evolving interlayer spacings were consistent with the XRD, Raman spectroscopy, and NEXAFS results.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs8184-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Behavior of Ball-Milled Graphite</h3>
            
            <p>For the electrochemical characterization of the samples, CR2032 coin-type cells were constructed using 4, 8, 12, and 24&nbsp;h-MG, with sodium metal as the working and counter electrodes in a carbonate-based electrolyte. Notably, various carbonate-based were used as solvent in SIB electrolytes. Among them, mixture of propylene carbonate (PC) and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) solvent exhibited the best cycle retention of 12&nbsp;h-MG at a current density of 1 A·g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Accordingly, in our study, NaPF<sub>6</sub> and PC:FEC were used as the salt and solvent, respectively. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs8184-fig-0003">3A</a> displays the voltage profiles of pristine graphite and the 4, 8, 12, and 24&nbsp;h-MG during the 2nd galvanostatic charge-discharge cycle at a current density of 100 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>. The monotonic sloping curves without a plateau region observed across all samples signify varied reaction mechanisms akin to hard carbon. The pristine graphite exhibited a considerably reduced reversible capacity of 21.1 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup>, aligning with prior findings. The 4&nbsp;h-MG exhibited an enhanced reversible capacity of 142.7 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup>. The 12&nbsp;h-MG delivered the highest reversible capacity of 269.7 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> among the ball-milled graphite samples. Conversely, the reversible capacity for the 24&nbsp;h-MG decreased to 208.8 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup>. While the 4&nbsp;h-MG possessed the largest BET surface area, the 12&nbsp;h-MG exhibited the maximum reversible capacity. Although all milled graphite electrodes displayed similar voltage profiles, their reversible capacities were not proportional to the milling time. Carbonaceous materials with sloping voltage profiles store sodium ions through surface-dominant reactions, such as insertion/extraction and adsorption. Consequently, the charge/discharge behavior of graphite strongly indicates that the reversible capacity is affected by the surface area and additional factors. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0003">3B</a> illustrates the cycling stability of pristine graphite and the 4, 8, 12, and 24&nbsp;h-MG at a current density of 100 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>. Although all samples exhibited decreased reversible capacities over the initial five cycles, they maintained stable cycling performance up to the 200th cycle. Since the 12&nbsp;h-MG consistently achieved the highest reversible capacity throughout all cycles, it was selected for further electrochemical evaluations. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">3C</a> shows the rate capability test results for the 12&nbsp;h-MG, demonstrating reversible capacities of 290.5, 254.3, 235.2, 222.6, 205.2, 176.6, and 157.7 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 10, 20, 50, 100, 200, 500, and 1000 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. After a discharge/charge cycle at a current density of 1 A·g<sup>−1</sup>, its capacity recovered to 237.6 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density was reduced to 10 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>. These results indicated the remarkable rate capability of the 12&nbsp;h-MG, sustaining a capacity retention of 52% despite a 100-fold increase in current density (from 10 mA·g<sup>−1</sup> to 1000 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">3D</a> presents the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves for the 12&nbsp;h-MG, recorded at escalating scan rates from 0.1 to 1 mV·s<sup>−1</sup>. The CV curves for the 12&nbsp;h-MG exhibited broad cathodic and anodic peaks with a minimal voltage offset of ≈0.2&nbsp;V. Moreover, the negligible peak shift with a 10-fold increase in the scan rate highlighted the superior electrochemical kinetics of the 12&nbsp;h-MG. The long-term cycling stability of the 12&nbsp;h-MG was evaluated at a high current density of 1 A·g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0003">3E</a>). Although a minor drop in capacity was observed at the 30<sup>th</sup> cycle, the high reversible capacity of 130 mAh·g<sup>−1</sup> was preserved up to the 3000th cycle despite the high current density (1 A·g<sup>−1</sup>). This indicates the robust cycle stability of the 12&nbsp;h-MG. The coulombic efficiencies following the 1st, 2nd, and 8th cycles were ≈40, 90, and 100%, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a8d17f28-ec19-4edc-8587-52c1bb23420c/advs8184-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a8d17f28-ec19-4edc-8587-52c1bb23420c/advs8184-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/95b0fa39-852a-4469-ad58-6e9f0c6c1b46/advs8184-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a8d17f28-ec19-4edc-8587-52c1bb23420c/advs8184-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance analysis. A) Potential profiles of ball-milled graphite with varying milling times (potential range: 0.01–3&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, current density: 100 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>). B) Cycle stability of the samples at a current density of 100 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>. C) Rate capability test for the 12&nbsp;h-MG over current densities from 10 to 1000 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>. D) Cycling voltammetry (CV) curves of 12&nbsp;h-MG at increasing scan rates from 0.1 to 1 mV·s<sup>−1</sup>. E) Long-term cycling stability test and coulombic efficiency of 12&nbsp;h-MG with coulombic efficiency at a high current density of 1 A·g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
