         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs8184-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodium-Ion Storage Mechanism of Ball-Milled Graphite</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To investigate the sodium-ion reaction mechanism within graphite, 4 and 12&nbsp;h-MG were further analyzed. This was because the 4&nbsp;h-MG had a higher BET surface area than the 12&nbsp;h-MG, while the 12&nbsp;h-MG presented more defects than the 4&nbsp;h-MG (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). When examining the internal structures of these samples, the d-spacing of the 4&nbsp;h-MG slightly deviated from that of graphite, while the d-spacing in the 12&nbsp;h-MG was larger than that in graphite. Consequently, 4 and 12&nbsp;h-MG were compared to determine the factors influencing the sodium-ion storage reaction mechanism (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Sodium-ion storage can occur through either a bulk diffusion-limited reaction or a surface-oriented capacitive reaction, contingent upon the reaction site. The relative contributions of these mechanisms can be discerned from CV measurements at increasing scan rates according to the following formula:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="advs8184-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/416e2a00-a989-48bd-bbdd-8961ae3acae1/advs8184-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs8184:advs8184-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/advs8184-math-0004.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              </mrow>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>ν</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{i}} = {\mathrm{a\ }}{{{{\nu}}}^{\mathrm{b}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>where i denotes the current, ν is the scan rate, and b holds values of 0.5 and 1 for bulk-diffusion limited reactions and surface-oriented capacitive reactions, respectively. According to the CV curves of 4 and 12&nbsp;h-MG (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5B,D</a>, Supporting Information), the b values for the 12&nbsp;h-MG (0.85 and 0.88, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5E</a>, Supporting Information) were greater than those for the 4&nbsp;h-MG (0.73 and 0.83, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5C</a>, Supporting Information). This demonstrates a surface-focused reaction mechanism for sodium ions in the 12&nbsp;h-MG. However, the 12&nbsp;h-MG had a smaller BET surface area than the 4&nbsp;h-MG. Therefore, defects in graphite have a more pronounced impact on surface reactions than the surface area. These results align with previous studies on hard-carbon-based SIB anodes, highlighting that surface defects can enhance sodium ion storage, resulting in an increased capacity in the sloping region.</div>
            
            <p>Ex situ XPS depth analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) was performed to elucidate the sodium-ion reaction mechanism within the surface and bulk regions of the samples. The sodiated/desodiated coin cells were placed in a glove box under an inert atmosphere and transferred to an XPS chamber under Ar protection. Each sample underwent Ar<sup>+</sup> ion beam etching, with XPS repeated at 1 min intervals.</p>
            
            <p>These results align with previous studies on hard-carbon-based SIB anodes, highlighting that surface defects can enhance sodium ion storage, resulting in an increased capacity in the sloping region. Moreover, it indicates that oxygen atoms participate in the reversible sodium ion storage reaction in the bulk region. XPS analysis was performed to analyze the reaction.</p>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0004">4</a> shows the ex situ XPS spectra deconvolutions for the 12&nbsp;h-MG through a depth analysis. The gray, solid, and dotted lines denote the pristine samples before the electrochemical reaction, the sodiation state (0.01&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), and the desodiation state (3&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), respectively. The depth profiles of the C<sub>1s</sub> spectra for the 12&nbsp;h-MG at the surface and after 10, 20, 30, and 40&nbsp;min of etching are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0004">4A</a>. The surface of the pristine 12&nbsp;h-MG exhibited a typical disordered carbon structure with oxygen functional groups. Additionally, three distinct peaks at 284.5, 286, and 290&nbsp;eV were observed, corresponding to C─C, C─O, and C─F (polyvinylidene fluoride, PVDF), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0025_R_d82019359e1142" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Electrochim. Acta">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These peaks vanished post-sodiation owing to the insertion of sodium and oxygen atoms into the carbon matrix. However, these carbon peaks reappeared in the desodiation state. No peaks indicative of direct Na-C bonds were detected. The O<sub>1s</sub> spectra remained consistent through the sodiation/desodiation processes, as evidenced in the C<sub>1s</sub> spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0004">4B</a>). The intensity of the peak at ≈ 532&nbsp;eV, corresponding to ─O bonding in the bulk region, increased after sodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0026_R_d82019359e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Carbon">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the carbon-oxygen peak (286&nbsp;eV) was not observed in the sodiated state in the C<sub>1s</sub> spectra. Therefore, the O<sub>1s</sub> spectra indicated the absence of oxygen bonding. Accordingly, the peaks were inferred to represent sodium-oxygen (Na─O) bonds, reminiscent of those in Na<sub>2</sub>O. The peak at 530.1&nbsp;eV was assigned to sodium carbonate (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), which was observed on the surface of the sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0027_R_d82019359e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The F<sub>1s</sub> spectrum of the surface for the pristine 12&nbsp;h-MG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0004">4C</a>) shows a distinct peak at 687.6&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the C-F bond in PVDF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0028_R_d82019359e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Power Sources">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While most peaks disappeared after sodiation, the peak at 683.7&nbsp;eV reappeared post-desodiation. This is attributed to the reversible formation of sodium fluoride (NaF), which will be elaborated upon using ex situ NEXAFS spectroscopy. The F<sub>1s</sub> spectrum of the bulk region exhibited a stable peak at 685.1&nbsp;eV, confirming the presence of an inorganic trapped sodium compound. The Na<sub>1s</sub> spectra of the surface of the 12&nbsp;h-MG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0004">4D</a>) were deconvoluted to distinguish the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (1071.7&nbsp;eV) and metallic Na (1071.0&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0029_R_d82019359e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Carbon">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These peaks suggested the reversible formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface of the 12&nbsp;h-MG during charging. The metallic sodium peak was correlated with sodium adsorption onto the micropores in the low-potential region. The Na<sub>1s</sub> spectrum of the bulk region revealed a reversible peak at 1072.6&nbsp;eV that corresponds to Na<sub>2</sub>O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0030_R_d82019359e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Non-Cryst. Solids">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the bulk reaction within the 12&nbsp;h-MG resulted in the formation of Na-O compounds without involving sodium ion intercalation. The proposed mechanism was evaluated using ex situ XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). No shift was observed in the position of the (002) peak in the sodiated or desodiated states, suggesting that sodium ions did not intercalate between the graphite layers.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3bbbb72e-f1e2-4cc8-b347-81a9d05ab390/advs8184-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3bbbb72e-f1e2-4cc8-b347-81a9d05ab390/advs8184-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c80b8ce9-3aeb-49cd-a8f4-8b1090839ebf/advs8184-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3bbbb72e-f1e2-4cc8-b347-81a9d05ab390/advs8184-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS depth profile of 12&nbsp;h-MG with Ar<sup>+</sup> ion etching from surface to 40 mins etched: A) C<sub>1s</sub>, B) O<sub>1s</sub>, C) F<sub>1s</sub>, and D) Na<sub>1s</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Ex situ XPS analysis confirmed the primary reaction between ball-milled graphite and sodium ions occurs at the surface. However, accurately analyzing these surface reactions with XPS is challenging. As a result, we employed ex situ NEXAFS spectroscopy for detailed surface reaction analysis. NEXAFS provides a robust method for assessing the chemical state of surfaces, and its energy range is aptly suited for examining carbon and oxygen, crucial elements in carbon-based sodium-ion battery anodes. The milled graphite samples underwent galvanostatic charging/discharging under a current density of 50 mA·g<sup>−1</sup>, subsequently transferred to the NEXAFS chamber under an argon atmosphere to prevent air exposure. The electrode was fabricated without a carbon-conducting agent to minimize the detection of unrelated chemical bonds. NEXAFS measurements were performed in the total electron yield (TEY) mode, targeting a detection depth of ≈2&nbsp;nm. The NEXAFS carbon K-edge spectra during the initial sodiation phase of pristine graphite, 4&nbsp;h-MG, and 12&nbsp;h-MG were measured and compared, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). The powder NEXAFS spectra of the samples before sodiation revealed two predominant peaks corresponding to the σ and π bonds (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0001">1E</a>). The intensities of these peaks gradually diminished during the initial sodiation step, eventually vanishing upon sodiation completion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10B</a>, Supporting Information). New peaks emerged at ≈288 and 290&nbsp;eV, superseding the peaks previously corresponding to the σ and π bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0023_R_d82019359e1266" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Electron Spectrosc. Relat. Phenom.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the pristine graphite was completely sodiated to 0.01&nbsp;V, the intensities of σ<sup>*</sup> and π<sup>*</sup> peaks negligibly changed, and faint new peaks became discernible. Conversely, the σ<sup>*</sup> and π<sup>*</sup> peaks of the 4&nbsp;h-MG rapidly vanished upon sodiation, and distinct new peaks appeared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10C</a>, Supporting Information). A subtle distinction was observed between the carbon K-edge spectra of 4 and 12&nbsp;h-MG. Specifically, a diminutive initial peak of the 4&nbsp;h-MG persisted post-complete sodiation, whereas, for the 12&nbsp;h-MG, all original peaks disappeared. This suggested that the 12&nbsp;h-MG underwent greater electrochemical activation than the 4&nbsp;h-MG. Additionally, the 4&nbsp;h-MG showed two sharp peaks of comparable intensities at 0.38&nbsp;V, while for the 12&nbsp;h-MG, the peak intensity at 290&nbsp;eV was higher than that at 288&nbsp;eV. The energy range between 288.5 and 290.2&nbsp;eV in the NEXAFS spectra is still not fully identified.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0031_R_d82019359e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The origin of these two peaks is discussed in the subsequent section, drawing comparisons with control samples and previous reports. The peaks at 288.5 and 290.2&nbsp;eV corresponded to oxalate (deriving from sodium oxalate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) and carbonate (originating from Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), respectively, principal constituents of the SEI layer. When the 12&nbsp;h-MG was sodiated to 0.8&nbsp;V during the first cycle, the oxalate and carbonate peaks were prominent, signifying the SEI layer formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0032_R_d82019359e1300" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Carbon">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The oxygen K-edge spectra mirrored the carbon K-edge spectra during the first cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10D–F</a>, Supporting Information). The 4 and 12&nbsp;h-MG showed similar sharp peaks at 533&nbsp;eV and broad peaks spanning from 539–545&nbsp;eV. Conversely, the pristine graphite exhibited peaks of minimal intensity. These peaks were assigned to Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (532.9 and 544&nbsp;eV) and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (534.6 and 539.8&nbsp;eV), stemming from the SEI layer formation on the sample surfaces. Furthermore, the oxygen k-edge spectra of the 4&nbsp;h-MG showed sharp peaks at 1.5&nbsp;V, whereas the 12&nbsp;h-MG exhibited a less-defined peak. This irregularity in the latter was ascribed to an incompletely formed SEI, hinting at the correlation between SEI formation and the sample surface area. Since the 4&nbsp;h-MG possessed the highest surface area, it readily formed an SEI layer on its surface. Accordingly, the abundant SEI layer on the 4&nbsp;h-MG resulted in the lowest initial efficiency among the samples. However, the fluorine K-edge spectra exhibited characteristics distinct from the carbon and oxygen K-edge spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10G–I</a>, Supporting Information). For instance, the fluorine K-edge spectra displayed the highest and lowest intensities at 0.8 and 0.01&nbsp;V, respectively. This pattern contrasts with the behaviors observed in the carbon and oxygen spectra.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the chemical composition on the surface of graphite, Ex situ NEXAFS were performed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0005">5</a>. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0005">5A</a>, the carbon K-edge spectra of the sodiated and desodiated electrodes (pristine graphite, 4&nbsp;h-MG, and 12&nbsp;h-MG) are juxtaposed with various carbon-based reference materials. The sharp peak at 288.5&nbsp;eV for the sodiated and desodiated electrodes was attributed to oxalate (derived from Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), differentiating them from the carbon K-edge spectra of the reference materials. Furthermore, this stable oxalate peak remained consistent in intensity in the sodiated and desodiated states. The peak at 290.2&nbsp;eV was assigned to the carbonate bond, displaying varied intensities pre and post-sodiation across all samples. Therefore, the peak was inferred to contribute to the energy storage mechanism. In this study, the 12&nbsp;h-MG exhibited more pronounced peak intensity changes than the 4&nbsp;h-MG and pristine graphite, suggesting that defects contribute more to electrochemical activity than the surface area. Moreover, the carbonate peak shifted to 289.6&nbsp;eV, indicating an elongation in the atomic bond length of the carbonate. Therefore, the bonds might weaken compared to those in pristine Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8184-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8184-bib-0024_R_d82019359e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the peak position for the nonreactive oxalate was identical to that of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0005">5B</a> shows the oxygen K-edge spectra of the samples. The primary peak at 534.3&nbsp;eV for the 12&nbsp;h-MG was assigned to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. The red shift of the peak provides evidence of an increased bond length in the carbonate. The peak at 532.9&nbsp;eV originated from Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, while the broad peaks at 539 and 545&nbsp;eV were attributed to carbonate and oxalate, respectively. The 12&nbsp;h-MG demonstrated distinct peak positions in the sodiation and desodiation states. The peak intensity increased during sodiation and decreased in desodiation, similar to the carbon spectra. Conversely, the oxygen K-edge spectra of pristine graphite exhibited low-intensity peaks owing to the subdued reactivity of sodium ions. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0005">5C</a> displays the fluorine K-edge spectra of the samples. The distinct peak at 690.2&nbsp;eV was indexed to NaF. However, the NEXAFS findings for fluorine markedly diverged from those for other elements. The intensity at 690.2&nbsp;eV decreased during sodiation and increased during desodiation. The peak intensity fluctuations for the 12&nbsp;h-MG were considerably greater than those for the 4&nbsp;h-MG. The ex situ NEXAFS results primarily revealed variations in the spectral peaks based on the state of charge (SoC) of the ball-milled graphite. These peak shifts signified chemical reactions on the SEI surface, given the measurement depth for the TEY mode in NEXAFS spectroscopy was 2&nbsp;nm. Therefore, the NEXAFS results confirmed the formation of an SEI layer on ball-milled graphite comprising three main chemicals: Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and NaF. These three components undergo distinct reactions during the charge/discharge cycles. Particularly, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> exhibited a low reactivity akin to that of a conventional SEI layer, whereas NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> yielded divergent outcomes. These results were strongly related to the structural and morphological properties of the samples brought about by the ball-milling duration and resonated with their electrochemical performance. Therefore, we advocate that sodium-ion storage is favored on defect-rich carbon surfaces.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/81e5f138-1a64-43b3-8755-b3cecf2724dc/advs8184-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/81e5f138-1a64-43b3-8755-b3cecf2724dc/advs8184-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e394d0c5-04b5-42bc-b9e7-2ce8e19d014e/advs8184-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/81e5f138-1a64-43b3-8755-b3cecf2724dc/advs8184-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ NEXAFS measurement at the 2nd sodiation/desodiation state of 12h-MG with reference materials. A) Carbon K-edge, B) oxygen K-edge, and C) fluorine K-edge. Solid lines represent the fully sodiated condition, while dashed lines correspond to the desodiated state.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0006">6</a> presents the ex situ NEXAFS measurements for the 12&nbsp;h-MG, showcasing A) carbon K-edge and B) oxygen K-edge spectra at various sodiation/desodiation states during the 2nd cycle. The carbonate peaks of 12&nbsp;h-MG demonstrated robust reversibility through repeated charge/discharge cycles. In this study, the voltage range where the carbonate peak was altered was confirmed by determining the relative ratio of the reactive carbonate peak to the stable oxalate peak (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0006">6D</a>). The findings suggested that the carbonate peak changes for the 12&nbsp;h-MG were greater than those for the 4&nbsp;h-MG in the low-voltage region. This emphatically pointed to defect-driven surface reactions within the low-potential region. Extensive ex situ NEXAFS analysis revealed that the reversible reaction did not originate from an initial side reaction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0006">6E</a>). Furthermore, the carbonate peak maintained strong reversibility for up to 25 cycles. Additionally, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) shows the NEXAFS spectra of the sodiated and desodiated electrodes, highlighting the surface reactions during discharging and charging.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/99298c26-de76-498f-a218-1520a54ac70c/advs8184-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/99298c26-de76-498f-a218-1520a54ac70c/advs8184-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cee7636e-9ac2-4890-86cc-3b0e096f2201/advs8184-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/99298c26-de76-498f-a218-1520a54ac70c/advs8184-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ NEXAFS measurements of 12&nbsp;h-MG in each voltage step during sodiation and desodiation. A) Carbon K-edge and B) oxygen K-edge curves. C) Galvanostatic cycling profile at the 2<sup>nd</sup> sodiation/desodiation cycle. D) Relative intensity between carbonate and oxalate peaks as a function of cell voltage. E) Carbon K-edge of 12&nbsp;h-MG during long-term cycling.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Ex situ TEM analysis was performed on sodiated 12&nbsp;h-MG to further investigate the reaction mechanism of ball-milled graphite. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0007">7B–E</a> shows the focused ion beam (FIB)-TEM images using high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). Sodium ions were detected on the carbonaceous particle surfaces and within the bulk of the ball-milled graphite. Therefore, the 12&nbsp;h-MG engaged in surface and bulk reactions with sodium ions. The HR-TEM image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11A</a>, Supporting Information) of the sodiated 12&nbsp;h-MG showed nanocrystalline lattice fringes assigned to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF. The presence of active sites on the SEI surface (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF) was confirmed. Nanocrystalline lattice fringes without disrupted carbon fringes were not observed in the HR-TEM images of the bare 12&nbsp;h-MG (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11B</a>, Supporting Information). Elemental distributions across the carbon surface were assessed using TEM-EDS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11C–F</a>, Supporting Information). Carbon, oxygen, sodium, and fluorine ions were uniformly distributed on the surface of the 12&nbsp;h-MG. Before the electrochemical reaction, fluorine was absent from the bare electrode surface. However, post-reaction, the TEM-EDS image displays a uniform fluorine coating on the surface, confirming the presence of NaF on the carbon surface. The structural changes in 12&nbsp;h-MG during sodiation and desodiation were investigated using in situ Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The intensity of the D peak decreased during sodiation, reverting to its original intensity following desodiation.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/be8b7c79-a2ec-452c-9863-74bf227b5843/advs8184-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/be8b7c79-a2ec-452c-9863-74bf227b5843/advs8184-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/684363ea-e033-4830-a5e0-ceb799222b79/advs8184-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/be8b7c79-a2ec-452c-9863-74bf227b5843/advs8184-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cross-sectional TEM analysis. A) Schematic illustration of cross-section sample preparation. B) Scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) image of the cross-section of 12&nbsp;h-MG. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images for C) carbon, D) sodium, and E) oxygen elements.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>This result contrasts with the ex situ Raman spectra, which indicate the presence of chemically enhanced EG and hard carbon with a high D/G ratio after sodiation. These analyses demonstrate increased structural randomness through the intercalation or insertion of sodium ions into the carbon layer. However, ex situ XRD results indicated that the sodium ion intercalation reaction did not occur within the ball-milled graphite layers. In situ Raman spectroscopy also indicated a potential intercalation reaction. Consequently, we contend that the sodium-ion storage mechanism within the graphite layer diverges from prior findings. This necessitates further investigation into graphite as an anode material in practical settings.</p>
            
            <p>We outlined the proposed reaction mechanism for ball-milled graphite based on the results from various analyses (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0008">8</a>). The sodium-ion storage reaction between the ball-milled graphite and sodium ions encompasses surface and bulk reactions. Surface reactions involve sodium ion adsorption onto the carbon, primarily characterizing hard carbon in the sloping-voltage region. The X-ray analyses conducted in this research did not identify any adsorption reactions. Furthermore, the NEXAFS measurements verified the occurrence of an unanticipated reversible reaction on the SEI surface of the defect-rich structure. The ex situ XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) and in situ Raman analyses (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) indicated that the bulk reaction of ball-milled graphite was not an intercalation reaction. Instead, sodium ions are introduced into the graphite layer through the formation of Na─O compounds. This bulk reaction is significantly affected by the spacing between graphite layers. When the d-spacing was insufficient, sodium ions were irreversibly trapped within the graphite layer, lowering the initial efficiency.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8184-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1db9393d-20e5-4ddc-9b76-0d0c3b825169/advs8184-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1db9393d-20e5-4ddc-9b76-0d0c3b825169/advs8184-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f340db18-113a-4a14-8cc3-51295380a154/advs8184-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1db9393d-20e5-4ddc-9b76-0d0c3b825169/advs8184-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8184-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202401022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the proposed reaction mechanism for A) pristine graphite, B) 4&nbsp;h-MG, and C) 12&nbsp;h-MG.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0008">8A</a> illustrates the reaction of pristine graphite with sodium ions, revealing a subdued surface reaction with irreversibly trapped sodium compounds in the graphite layers. While the surface reaction in 4&nbsp;h-MG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0008">8B</a>) was facilitated by adsorption, the bulk region experienced irreversible Na─O compound formation owing to insufficient d-spacing. Finally, the 12&nbsp;h-MG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8184-fig-0008">8C</a>) exhibited the most dominant surface reaction owing to the reversible formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF. Furthermore, the increased interlayer spacing enabled the reversible formation of Na─O compounds, facilitating the bulk reaction in the 12&nbsp;h-MG. However, the detailed mechanism of the bulk reaction remains elusive and warrants further investigation. We anticipate that our findings will provide a foundational perspective on the reaction mechanism of slope-dominant carbon anodes, lacking a distinct plateau region.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8184-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8184-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We explored the mechanical activation of graphite for use as an SIB anode through controlled ball milling. By comprehensively investigating the impact of mechanical energy, variations in the structural and morphological properties we observed and their effects on electrochemical performance relative to milling time were analyzed. The sodium-ion storage mechanism was investigated using ex situ NEXAFS, XPS, TEM, XRD, and in situ RAMAN. The results from these analyses confirmed the simultaneous occurrence of surface and bulk reactions in the defect-rich ball-milled graphite. During sodiation, sodium ions were initially adsorbed on the surface of ball-milled graphite. Furthermore, the reversible reactions on the SEI surface resulted in alternating formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF owing to the defect-rich carbon surface. This served as an auxiliary sodium reservoir, enhancing carbon anode performance. In the bulk region, sodium ions integrated through adequate EG layers, forming Na─O compounds. The identified sodium ion storage mechanism significantly diverged from intercalation materials featuring a plateau region, such as hard carbon. This unprecedented storage mechanism provides novel insights for the low-cost development of slope-dominant carbon electrodes with defect-rich structures. This study provides a meticulous examination of the mechanical activation of graphite for high-performance, cost-effective sodium ion storage. Furthermore, it introduces a previously uncharted reaction mechanism for defect-rich carbon electrodes, marked by an unexpected reversible reaction on the SEI surface.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8184-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8184-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8184-sec-0100-title"> Synthesis of x h-MG</h4>
                  
                  <p>Mechanically activated graphite was prepared using a straightforward ball-milling process. A planetary ball-milling machine (Pulverisette 6, Fritsch) was employed to induce mechanical impact. 10&nbsp;g of graphite (Sigma–Aldrich) was placed in a zirconia vessel containing 200&nbsp;g of zirconia balls (9&nbsp;mm diameter). The vessel was securely sealed under dry air to prevent potential effects from moisture and then agitated at 400&nbsp;rpm. After the milling, the vessel was cautiously opened in a dry environment.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8184-sec-0110-title"> Material Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>X-ray data were collected at the HRPD 9B beamline of the Pohang Accelerator Laboratory (PAL). Raman spectra were measured using a 532&nbsp;nm laser (Renishaw, England). NEXAFS was conducted at the 10D beamline of PAL to analyze chemical state variations. The surface area was analyzed through nitrogen adsorption-desorption using the BET method (Autosorb-iQ 2ST/MP, Quantachrome). The chemical bonding characteristics were evaluated using XPS. Morphological changes in the ball-milled graphite were observed using FE-SEM (Inspect F, Thermo Fisher). The microstructural collapse was examined through HR-TEM (Titan, Thermofisher).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8184-sec-0120-title"> Electrochemical Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>A composite slurry was prepared from a mixture of ball-milled graphite, Denka black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP, Alfa Aesar) with an 8:1:1 weight ratio. The slurry was cast onto copper foil (Welcos, Korea) and dried in a vacuum oven at 80&nbsp;°C. The typical active material loading was 1.2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The coin cell was fabricated using ball-milled graphite as the working electrode, sodium metal as the counter electrode, a glass fiber separator (Whatman), and a 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte in a PC:FEC solvent mixture (98:2 weight ratio). Fabrication was conducted in a glove box under an Ar atmosphere. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed using a MACCOR series 4000 battery tester after resting for 12&nbsp;h, within a potential range of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V (relative to Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). CV evaluations were conducted using a VMP3 instrument (Bio-logic Science Instruments) at varying scan rates.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8184-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8184-sec-0130-title"> Ex Situ Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>For ex situ analysis, electrodes were prepared without Denka black (a conducting agent) to minimize side reactions. After cycling, the coin cell was disassembled in an Ar-filled glove box. The electrode was subsequently rinsed with a pure solvent to remove residual electrolyte. This cleaned electrode was placed on a NEXAFS holder inside the glove box and transferred to a NEXAFS chamber within the 10D beamline at PAL, ensuring no air exposure. Ex situ NEAXAFS was performed in the TEY and fluorescence (FY) modes. For ex situ XPS depth profiling, the cleaned electrode was placed on an XPS holder and transferred to the XPS chamber in a glove box, maintaining an air- and moisture-free environment. Ar<sup>+</sup> ion sputtering etching and X-ray measurements were alternated at 1-min intervals. Cross-sectional samples for TEM analysis were prepared using FIB milling (Quanta 3D, Thermo Fisher). To prevent contact with air and moisture, a vacuum transfer holder was used for transferring samples between the FIB and TEM systems. TEM-EDS (energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometry) mapping was performed using a Talos TEM (Thermo Fisher Scientific). Selected area diffraction (SAED) patterns were sourced from a JEM-ARM 200F TEM device. The in situ Raman spectra during the 1st sodiation/desodiation were gathered using a Raman instrument (Renishaw, UK).</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
